</ul>
<p>On the same motorway, a long-term test of a <a href="/wiki/Guided_bus" title="Guided bus">guided bus</a> system is being held since 1980. Many Ruhrbahn rail lines meet at the main station but only a handful of bus lines. However, all but one of the Nacht Express bus lines originate from / lead to Essen Hauptbahnhof in a star-shaped manner. All Ruhrbahn lines, including the Nacht Express lines, are closed on weekdays from 1:30&#160;a.m. to 4:30&#160;a.m.</p>
<p>Of the <a href="/wiki/Rhein-Ruhr_S-Bahn" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhein-Ruhr S-Bahn">Rhein-Ruhr S-Bahn</a> net's 13 lines, 5 lines lead through Essen territory and meet at the <a href="/wiki/Essen_Hauptbahnhof" title="Essen Hauptbahnhof">Essen Hauptbahnhof</a> main station, which also serves as the connection to the <a href="/wiki/Regional-Express" title="Regional-Express">Regional-Express</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intercity-Express" title="Intercity-Express">Intercity-Express</a> network of regional and nationwide high-speed trains, respectively. Following Essen's appointment as <a href="/wiki/European_Capital_of_Culture" title="European Capital of Culture">European Capital of Culture</a> 2010, the main station, which is classified as a <a href="/wiki/German_railway_station_categories#Category_1" title="German railway station categories">station of highest importance</a> and which had not been substantially renovated over decades, will be redeveloped with a budget of €57 million until early 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Other important stations in Essen, where regional and local traffic are connected, are the Regionalbahnhöfe (regional railway stations) in the boroughs of Altenessen, <a href="/wiki/Essen-Borbeck-Mitte" title="Essen-Borbeck-Mitte">Borbeck</a>, Kray and Steele. Further 20 S-Bahn stations can be found in the whole urban area.</p>
<p>In 2017 the public transport organization of Mülheim, the Mülheimer Verkehrsgesellschaft (MVG) and the Essener Verkehrsgesellschaft (EVAG) merged and became the <i>Ruhrbahn</i>. All vehicles and staff were merged and are now operated together.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aviation">Aviation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Aviation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG/220px-Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG/330px-Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG/440px-Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1945" data-file-height="1284" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Luftbild_Flughafen_Essen-M%C3%BClheim.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Essen/M%C3%BClheim_Airport" title="Essen/Mülheim Airport">Essen/Mülheim Airport</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Together with the neighbouring city of <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BClheim" title="Mülheim">Mülheim an der Ruhr</a> and the state of <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">North Rhine-Westphalia</a>, Essen maintains <a href="/wiki/Essen/M%C3%BClheim_Airport" title="Essen/Mülheim Airport">Essen/Mülheim Airport</a> (<a href="/wiki/IATA_airport_code" title="IATA airport code">IATA</a>: ESS, <a href="/wiki/ICAO_airport_code" title="ICAO airport code">ICAO</a>: EDLE). While the first flights had already arrived in 1919, it was officially opened on 25 August 1925. Significantly expanded in 1935, Essen/Mülheim became the central airport of the Ruhr area until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, providing an <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalted</a> runway of 1,553&#160;m (5,095&#160;ft), another unsurfaced runway for <a href="/wiki/Gliding" title="Gliding">gliding</a> and destinations to most major <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> cities. It was heavily damaged during the war, yet partly reconstructed and used by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> as a secondary airport since visibility is less often <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">obscured</a> than at <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf_Airport" title="Düsseldorf Airport">Düsseldorf Airport</a>. The latter then developed into the large civil airport that it is now, while Essen/Mülheim now mainly serves occasional air traffic (some 33,000 passengers each year),<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> the base of a fleet of <a href="/wiki/Airships" class="mw-redirect" title="Airships">airships</a> and Germany's oldest public <a href="/wiki/Flight_training" title="Flight training">flight training</a> company. Residents of the region around Essen typically use <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf_Airport" title="Düsseldorf Airport">Düsseldorf Airport</a> (~ 20 driving minutes) and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Dortmund_Airport" title="Dortmund Airport">Dortmund Airport</a> (~ 30 driving minutes) for both domestic and international flights.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zollverein_Industrial_Complex">Zollverein Industrial Complex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Zollverein Industrial Complex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zollverein_Coal_Mine_Industrial_Complex" title="Zollverein Coal Mine Industrial Complex">Zollverein Coal Mine Industrial Complex</a></div>
<p>The Zollverein Industrial Complex is the city's most famous landmark. For decades, the coal mine (current form mainly from 1932, closed in 1986) and the coking plant (closed in 1993) ranked among the largest of their kinds in Europe. Shaft XII, built in <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a> style, with its characteristic winding tower, which over the years has become a symbol for the whole <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a> area, is considered an architectural and technical masterpiece, earning it a reputation as the "most beautiful coal mine in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> had declared it a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> in 2001, the complex, which had lain idle for a long time and was even threatened to be demolished, began to see a period of redevelopment. Under the direction of an agency borne by the land of <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">North Rhine-Westphalia</a> and the city itself, several arts and design institutions settled mainly on the grounds of the former coal mine; a redevelopment plan for the coking plant is to be realised.</p>
<p>On the grounds of the coal mine and the coking plant, which are both accessible free of charge while paid guided tours (some with former <a href="/wiki/Miner" title="Miner">Kumpels</a>) are also available, several tourist attractions can be found, most importantly the <i>Design Zentrum NRW</i>/<a href="/wiki/Red_Dot" title="Red Dot">Red Dot Design Museum</a>. The <i>Ruhrmuseum</i>, a museum dedicated to the history of the Ruhr area, which had been existing since 1904, opened its gates as one of the anchor attractions in the former coal-washing facility in 2010.</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Essen_Minster_and_treasury">Essen Minster and treasury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Essen Minster and treasury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The former collegiate church of <a href="/wiki/Essen_Abbey" title="Essen Abbey">Essen Abbey</a> and nowadays cathedral of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Essen" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Essen">Bishop of Essen</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> hall church made from light <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>. The first church on the premises dates back to between 845 and 870; the current church was constructed after a former church had burnt down in 1275. However, the important <a href="/wiki/Westwork" title="Westwork">westwork</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crypt" title="Crypt">crypt</a> have survived from <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_architecture" title="Ottonian architecture">Ottonian times</a>. The cathedral is located in the centre of the city which evolved around it. It is not spectacular in appearance and the adjacent church <i>St. Johann Baptist</i>, which is located directly within the pedestrian precinct, is often mistakenly referred to as the cathedral. The cathedral treasury, however, ranks amongst the most important in Germany since only few art works have been lost over the centuries. The most precious exhibit, located within the cathedral, is the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Madonna_of_Essen" title="Golden Madonna of Essen">Golden Madonna of Essen</a> (around 980), the oldest known sculpture of the <a href="/wiki/Madonna_(art)" title="Madonna (art)">Madonna</a> and the oldest free-standing sculpture north of the Alps. Other exhibits include the alleged <i>child crown</i> of <a href="/wiki/Otto_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto III, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Otto III</a>, the eldest preserved seven-branched Christian <a href="/wiki/Candelabrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Candelabrum">candelabrum</a> and several other art works from Ottonian times.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Synagogue">Old Synagogue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Old Synagogue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Synagogue,_Essen" title="Old Synagogue, Essen">Old Synagogue, Essen</a></div>
<p>Opened in 1913, the then-<i>New Synagogue</i> served as the central meeting place of Essen's pre-war <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> community. The building ranks as one of the largest and most impressive testimonies of Jewish culture in pre-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">war</a> Germany. In post-war Germany, the former house of worship was bought by the city, used as an exhibition hall and later rededicated as a cultural meeting centre and house of Jewish culture.</p>

<h3><span id="Villa_Hügel"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Villa_H.C3.BCgel">Villa Hügel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Villa Hügel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Villa_H%C3%BCgel" title="Villa Hügel">Villa Hügel</a></div>
<p>Built in 1873 by industrial magnate <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Krupp" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred Krupp">Alfred Krupp</a>, the 269-room mansion (8,100&#160;m<sup>2</sup> or 87,190&#160;sq&#160;ft) and the surrounding park of 28&#160;ha (69.2 acres) served as the <a href="/wiki/Krupp" title="Krupp">Krupp</a> family's representative seat. The city's land register solely lists the property, which at times had a staff of up to 640 people, as a <a href="/wiki/Single-family_home" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-family home">single-family home</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> At the time of its construction, the villa featured some technical <a href="/wiki/Novelties" class="mw-redirect" title="Novelties">novelties</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peculiarity" class="mw-redirect" title="Peculiarity">peculiarities</a>, such as a central hot air heating system, own water- and <a href="/wiki/Gas_works" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas works">gas works</a> and electric internal and external <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a> systems (with a central <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">induction</a> alarm for the staff). The mansion's central <a href="/wiki/Clock" title="Clock">clock</a> became the reference clock for the whole <a href="/wiki/Krupp" title="Krupp">Krupp</a> enterprise; every clock was to be set with a maximum difference of half a minute. It even acquired its own <a href="/wiki/Railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Railway station">railway station</a>, <i>Essen Hügel</i>, which is still a regular stop. The Krupp family had to leave the <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%BCnderzeit" title="Gründerzeit">Gründerzeit</a> mansion in 1945, when it was annexed by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">allies</a>. Given back in 1952, Villa Hügel is now seat of the <a href="/wiki/Alfried_Krupp_von_Bohlen_und_Halbach" title="Alfried Krupp von Bohlen und Halbach">Alfried Krupp von Bohlen und Halbach</a> Foundation (major shareholder of Thyssen-Krupp) and was opened for concerts and sporadic yet high-profile exhibitions.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kettwig_and_Werden">Kettwig and Werden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Kettwig and Werden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the south of the city, the boroughs of Kettwig and Werden exceptionally stand for towns once of their own, which have been annexed in 1929 (Werden) and 1975 (Kettwig), respectively, and which have largely preserved their pre-annexation character. While most of the northern boroughs were heavily damaged during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> and often lost their historic town centres; the more southern parts got off more lightly.</p>
<p>In Werden, St. <a href="/wiki/Ludger" title="Ludger">Ludger</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Werden_Abbey" title="Werden Abbey">Werden Abbey</a> around 799, 45 years before St. <a href="/wiki/Altfrid" title="Altfrid">Altfrid</a> founded the later cornerstone of the modern city, <a href="/wiki/Essen_Abbey" title="Essen Abbey">Essen Abbey</a>. The old church of Werden abbey, <i>St. Ludgerus</i>, was designated a <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">papal</a> <a href="/wiki/Basilica_minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica minor">basilica minor</a> in 1993, while the main building of the former abbey today is the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/Folkwang_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Folkwang University">Folkwang University</a> of music and performing arts.</p>
<p>Kettwig, which was annexed in 1975, much to the dismay of the population that still struggles for independence,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> was mainly shaped by the <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a>. The most southern borough of Essen is also the city's largest (with regard to area) and presumably greenest.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_important_cultural_sites">Other important cultural sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Other important cultural sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Museum_Folkwang" title="Museum Folkwang">Museum Folkwang</a>: One of the Ruhr area's major art collections, mainly from the 19th and 20th centuries. Major parts of the museum have recently been rebuilt and expanded according to plans by <a href="/wiki/David_Chipperfield" title="David Chipperfield">David Chipperfield</a> &amp; Co. The Alfried Krupp von Bohlen und Halbach Foundation is the sole funder of the €55 million project which was completed in early 2010. After its re-opening, it also hosts the collection of the <i>Deutsches <a href="/wiki/Poster" title="Poster">Plakat</a> Museum</i> (more than 340 000 exhibits).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aalto_Theatre" title="Aalto Theatre">Aalto Theatre</a>: Opened in 1988 (the plans dating back to 1959), the <a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetric</a> building with its deep <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a> interior is home to the acclaimed Essen Opera and Ballet.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saalbau_Essen" title="Saalbau Essen">Saalbau Essen</a>: Home of the Essen Philharmonic <a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">Orchestra</a>, completely renovated in 2003/2004. Critics have repeatedly voted the Essen Philharmonic as Germany's Orchestra of the Year.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Colosseum_Theater" title="Colosseum Theater">Colosseum Theater</a>: Situated in a former Krupp factory building at the fringe of the central pedestrian precinct, the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Colosseum Theater</span> has been home to several <a href="/wiki/Musical_theatre" title="Musical theatre">musical theatre</a> productions since 1996.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zeche_Carl" title="Zeche Carl">Zeche Carl</a>, a former coal mine, now a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural centre">cultural centre</a> and venue for <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">Rock</a> concerts and home of <a href="/wiki/Offener_Kanal_Essen" title="Offener Kanal Essen">Offener Kanal Essen</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grillo-Theater" title="Grillo-Theater">Grillo-Theater</a>, a theatre in the centre of the city.</li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_sites">Other sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Other sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Garden_city_movement" title="Garden city movement">Gartenstadt</a> Margarethenhöhe: Founded by Margarethe Krupp in 1906, the garden city with its 3092 units in 935 buildings on an area of 115&#160;ha (284.2 acres) (of which 50 ha are woodland) is considered the first of its kind in Germany. All buildings follow the same stylistic concept, with slight variations for each one. Although originally designed as an area for the lower classes with quite small flats, the old part Margarethenhöhe I has developed into a middle class residential area and housing space has become highly sought after. A new part, Margarehenhöhe II, was built in the 1960s and 1970s but is architecturally inferior and especially the multi-storey buildings are still considered social hot spots.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Moltkeviertel" title="Moltkeviertel">Moltkeviertel</a> (Moltke Quarter): from 1908 on, following reformative plans of the city deputy <i>Robert Schmidt</i>, this quarter was developed just south-east of the city centre. Large green zones, forming broad urban ventilation lanes and incorporating sporting and playing areas and high quality architecture – invariably in the style of Reform Architecture, combine to create a unique example worldwide of modern town planning. It reflects reformative ideas and dates from the early part of the 20th century. The Moltkeviertel continues to be a much sought-after area for residential, educational, health care and small-scale commercial purposes. On the Moltkeplatz, the quarter's largest square, an ensemble of high quality contemporary art is maintained and cared for by local residents.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grugapark" title="Grugapark">Grugapark</a>: With a total area of 70&#160;ha (173.0 acres), the park near the exhibition halls is one of the largest urban parks in Germany and, although entry is not free of charge, one of the most popular recreational sites of the city. It includes the city's <a href="/wiki/Botanical_garden" title="Botanical garden">botanical garden</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Botanischer_Garten_Grugapark" title="Botanischer Garten Grugapark">Botanischer Garten Grugapark</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baldeneysee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Baldeneysee (page does not exist)">Baldeneysee</a>: The largest of the six <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr_(river)" title="Ruhr (river)">River Ruhr</a>, situated in the south of the city, is another popular recreational area. It is used for <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rowing_(sport)" title="Rowing (sport)">rowing</a> and ship tours. The hilly and only lightly developed forest area around the lake, from which the Kettwig area is easily reachable, is also popular with <a href="/wiki/Hiking" title="Hiking">hikers</a>.</li>
</ul>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people">Notable people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Notable people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dd><i>For a comprehensive list of people who were born or acted/lived in Essen, see <a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liste_von_Pers%C3%B6nlichkeiten_der_Stadt_Essen" class="extiw" title="de:Liste von Persönlichkeiten der Stadt Essen">this article in the German Wikipedia</a>.</i></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honorary_citizens">Honorary citizens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Essen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Honorary citizens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city of Essen has been awarding honorary citizenships since 1879 but has (coincidentally) discontinued this tradition after the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Republic of Germany">Federal Republic of Germany</a> in 1949. A notable exception was made in 2007, when Berthold Beitz, the president of the Alfried Krupp von Bohlen und Halbach Foundation received honorary citizenship for his long lasting commitment to the city.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The following list contains all honorary citizens of the city of Essen:<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>1879 <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor of Germany</a></li>
<li>1888 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Hammacher&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Friedrich Hammacher (page does not exist)">Friedrich Hammacher</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Hammacher" class="extiw" title="de:Friedrich Hammacher">de</a>)</span>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">politician</a>, lawyer and economist</li>
<li>1895 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Johann_Heinrich_Peter_Beising&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Johann Heinrich Peter Beising (page does not exist)">Johann Heinrich Peter Beising</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_Peter_Beising" class="extiw" title="de:Johann Heinrich Peter Beising">de</a>)</span>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Roman_catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman catholic">Roman catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologian</a></li>
<li>1896 <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Alfred_Krupp" title="Friedrich Alfred Krupp">Friedrich Alfred Krupp</a>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Industrialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialist">industrialist</a> <i>(spouse of Margarethe Krupp, see below)</i></li>
<li>1901 Heinrich Carl Sölling&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Tradesman" title="Tradesman">tradesman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benefactor_(law)" title="Benefactor (law)">benefactor</a></li>
<li>1906 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_Zweigert&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Erich Zweigert (page does not exist)">Erich Zweigert</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erich_Zweigert" class="extiw" title="de:Erich Zweigert">de</a>)</span>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mayor">Lord Mayor</a> (1886-1906)</li>
<li>1912 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Margarethe_Krupp&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Margarethe Krupp (page does not exist)">Margarethe Krupp</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margarethe_Krupp" class="extiw" title="de:Margarethe Krupp">de</a>)</span>&#160;– benefactress <i>(spouse of Friedrich Alfred Krupp, see above)</i></li>
<li>1917 <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Field_Marshal_(Germany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Field Marshal (Germany)">Generalfeldmarschall</a> and army leader, later <a href="/wiki/President_of_Germany_(1919%E2%80%931945)" title="President of Germany (1919–1945)">President of Germany</a></li>
<li>1948 Viktor Niemeyer&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Councilman" class="mw-redirect" title="Councilman">councilman</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Posthumous_recognition" class="mw-redirect" title="Posthumous recognition">posthumous recognition</a>)</i></li>
<li>2007 <a href="/wiki/Berthold_Beitz" title="Berthold Beitz">Berthold Beitz</a>&#160;– president of the Alfried Krupp von Bohlen und Halbach Foundation</li>
</ul>
<p>Today, the highest award of the city is the <i>Ring of Honour</i>, which Berthold Beitz, for example, had already received in 1983. Other bearers of the Ring of Honour include Essen's former Lord Mayor and later <a href="/wiki/President_of_Germany" title="President of Germany">President of Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Heinemann" title="Gustav Heinemann">Gustav Heinemann</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Franz_Hengsbach" title="Franz Hengsbach">Franz Cardinal Hengsbach</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Essen" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Essen">Bishop of Essen</a>.</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">

</div>
</div>
<p>The biggest <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a> clubs in Essen are <a href="/wiki/Rot-Weiss_Essen" title="Rot-Weiss Essen">Rot-Weiss Essen</a> (Red-White Essen) and <a href="/wiki/Schwarz-Wei%C3%9F_Essen" title="Schwarz-Weiß Essen">Schwarz-Weiß Essen</a> (Black-White Essen). <a href="/wiki/Stadion_Essen" title="Stadion Essen">Stadion Essen</a>, its home stadium, is located in the north of Essen. Rot-Weiss Essen is playing in the fourth tier of the German football league system, <a href="/wiki/Regionalliga_West" title="Regionalliga West">Regionalliga West</a>, and Schwarz-Weiß Essen in the fifth tier, <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_Nordrhein-Westfalen" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberliga Nordrhein-Westfalen">Oberliga Nordrhein-Westfalen</a>. Other football clubs are <a href="/wiki/BV_Altenessen" title="BV Altenessen">BV Altenessen</a>, <a href="/wiki/TuS_Helene_Altenessen" title="TuS Helene Altenessen">TuS Helene Altenessen</a>, <a href="/wiki/SG_Essen-Sch%C3%B6nebeck" class="mw-redirect" title="SG Essen-Schönebeck">SG Essen-Schönebeck</a>.</p>
<p>The city's main basketball team is <a href="/wiki/ETB_Essen" class="mw-redirect" title="ETB Essen">ETB Essen</a>, currently called the ETB Wohnbau Baskets for sponsorship reasons. The team is one of the main teams in Germany's second division <a href="/wiki/ProA" title="ProA">ProA</a> and has attempted to move up to Germany's elite league <a href="/wiki/Basketball_Bundesliga" title="Basketball Bundesliga">Basketball Bundesliga</a>. The Baskets play their home games at the <a href="/wiki/Sportpark_am_Hallo" title="Sportpark am Hallo">Sportpark am Hallo</a>.</p>
<p>Essen hosted the final round of the FIBA <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket_1971" title="EuroBasket 1971">EuroBasket 1971</a>.</p>
